#### Chapter Eight

 THE WORKERS’ PARADOX: INDENTURED LABOUR IN THE QUEENSLAND SUGAR INDUSTRY TO 1920

 Kay Saunders

##### Queensland in the late nineteenth century was a society of paradox, containing within it very diverse cultural and economic elements. It possessed a classical sugar plantation system which emerged in the 1860s, matured in the early 1880s and spectacularly collapsed a decade later. But uncomfortable vestiges of it remained alongside a small farm/’central’ or cooperatively-owned mill system. A second paradox was that Queensland also possessed a pastoral, mining and agricultural economy. So sugar was but one segment, albeit an important one within this varied economy. Another anomaly was that Queensland, in the period after the conquest of the Aborigines, acquired a predominantly British-derived population which resided overwhelmingly in the urban centres. But unlike the other Australian colonies, Queensland also contained substantial and diverse non-white minorities who were located primarily in the vast coastal sugar regions.
 This chapter will trace the development of indentured labour in Queensland in the second half of the nineteenth century, with special focus upon the sugar industry. Another series of paradoxes present themselves, for the partial destruction of the classical plantation system produced a curious hybrid transitional structure with forms of unionised labour performed by white men. This unprecedented feature owed its existence to salient political as well as economic factors. So whilst Queensland by the early twentieth century had become one of Australia’s dependent states because of the minimal existence of secondary industry, the reliance upon unstable primary industries and chronic drought, it was nevertheless subsumed within the wider political entity of the Commonwealth of Australia after 1901. If Australia was to commit itself to a ‘White Australia’ policy, then the Queensland sugar industry could no longer employ Pacific Island and Asian workers. Queensland was thus a society of paradox both within the Australian and imperial contexts.


-----

##### The first important paradox can be traced to the original colony of New South Wales (NSW) in the early nineteenth century, which contained elements that linked it unequivocally to the slave colonies of the Empire. Since Queensland did not separate from NSW until 1859, it is necessary to examine the development of unfree labour in the parent colony in order to understand the patterns that evolved out of the original system. In 1809 provisions were announced to allow Indians and Chinese to enter the penal settlement of NSW as bonded servants.[1] But their labour was not called upon while adequate numbers of transported British felons were available. Convicts operated as a variant of the slave mode of production, where the ownership of material resources like land, equipment, livestock and grain and the labour power of the assigned felons were united, like slaves, within the forces of production.[2] Connell and Irving have recently and questionably argued that:

 The legal basis of assignment was decidedly Marxist, the convicts’ labour power being regarded as a kind of commodity that was granted to the settlers by the government which maintained (in theory) control over the convicts’ person.[3]

 This interpretation ignores several important factors. First, under the Transportation Acts of 1717 and 1824, the state, in the person of the governor, owned the labour services of transported felons. These could be maintained within the public infrastructure to build roads, bridges or used in the private sector as assigned convicts. Thus, the essential criterion of ownership was unambiguous. The masters could not extract labour permanently from felons, as they owned, via the transfer of the governor’s prerogative, ‘property in the services’ as distinct from ‘property in the person’ and only for the duration of the transportee’s assignment whilst undergoing colonial exile and punishment. This does not alter the structure of the master/ slave syndrome. Slavery does not depend upon its being an hereditary status or for durante-vitae. Secondly, the transfer of the convict’s labour power was not a commodity, in the sense of a wage labourer selling his/her labour power on an open market. The felon’s labour power was directed by the authority of the governor and in no sense could an assigned convict choose a master or barter for wages. Thus, the Australian experience was different from that of the West Indies in the early nineteenth century, where masters owned their slaves outright. Most importantly, the Australian


-----

##### system was also different because initial ownership was vested in the apparatus of the state. It was even futher complicated by the fact that the owners of capital were originally the military and civil administrators of the colony.
 Contemporary observers frequently recognised that Australian convictism was a variation of slavery; but as Governor George Arthur of Van Diemen’s Land (VDL) assured any wavering doubters in 1835:

 Convicts, it is true are not black but white - and they are fellow countrymen - but they are criminals and the master may draw from his knowledge of their crimes a sanction, quite as satisfactory as that arising from differences of colour for any severity he would practise against them.[4]

 Unlike slave societies in other areas of the British Empire, NSW concurrently contained the widespread slave-variant mode (assignment) and both indenture and wage labouring of emancipated felons and non-convict British immigrant workers. These two categories could not be totally controlled, despite the operation of the punitive Masters and Servants Acts. As transportation drew to a close in the late 1830s, masters, wanting to ensure a continuing cheap servile labour force, deluged the Secretary of State for the Colonies with petitions demanding its continuance: the wealthy and influential colonial capitalists, recognising that the Secretary of State for the Colonies, Lord Glenelg, was committed to the principle that NSW was to be henceforth the home of the British stock, undertook other measures to ensure the continuance of unfree forms of labour.
 In 1837, forty Indians were initially introduced into NSW on five year terms of servitude and two further contingents went to the newly-formed convict-free colony of South Australia and another to the Swan River Colony (later Western Australia) the following year. One pastoralist revealed to a select committee enquiring into immigration that in NSW by 1838 some 1203 Chinese and Indian shepherds had already been engaged by 111 masters, most of whom also used assigned British felons.[5] Though the Sydney Herald, one of NSW’s most influential newspapers, declaimed that ‘slavery could not exist in this colony [as] the British abhor it ... [and] would crush it’, nevertheless concerned colonists felt that the settlement could readily become ‘a kind of civilised Tunis’ or ‘an emporium of an Eastern slave-trade’.[6] Many settlers feared that NSW might become another Jamaica.


-----

##### Lord Glenelg, then Secretary of State for the Colonies, moreover warned Governor Gipps that extensive Asian indenture would deter British settlement, as working-class Britons would be reluctant to locate themselves in a settlement which contained racially distinct and ‘degraded elements’.[7]
 But viewed across the Empire, this basic conflict of interest between abstract policies formulated in Whitehall and the urgent demands of wealthy colonists in NSW for cheap coloured labour had far-reaching ramifications. Australian pastoralists in the years 1837 to 1839 were essentially instigating the very same procedures as the West Indian and Mauritian sugar planters who were trying desperately to counteract the effects of emancipation. Certainly, their motives and requirements corresponded perfectly - to obtain a numerically inexhaustible, inexpensive and docile workforce which could be regimented and assiduously exploited. Furthermore, both the West Indies and Mauritius, on the one hand, and NSW and VDL, on the other, had possessed systems of slavery, complete and unambiguous in the former instances and an incomplete variation in the latter. The objections of the Colonial Office, however forcefully presented, might have been disregarded by Australian pastoralists who were willing to finance Indian and Chinese indenture without official supervision or assistance. But the Indian regulations of 1839 effectively prevented any comprehensive implementation of earlier proposals,[8] and the depression in the wool industry in the early 1840s rendered further expansion unfeasible. Several contingents of Indians were introduced however to the Darling Downs and Burnett regions of pre-separation Queensland in 1844. In 1862 the newly separated colony of Queensland passed an act specifically designed to import Indian indentured servants on a large scale. Though few Indians actually came to this tropical colony, the pattern of non-European indenture was firmly established and endured right into the twentieth century. These patterns were abandoned in the wealthy colonies of NSW and Victoria, which, by the 1860s, maintained significant secondary industries in the urban areas.[9]
 But long before these industrial patterns emerged, NSW had predominantly a pastoral economy, based upon the labour of transported felons. In 1824 a secondary detention centre for recalcitrant and reconvicted felons was established in the remote Moreton Bay district of NSW where free white settlers were prohibited from entering. It was a particularly harsh and primitive penal settlement[10] which contributed little to the subsequent development of labour patterns, for it did not permit assignment. In the early


-----

##### 1840s, when this penal settlement was still operating, wealthy pastoralists illegally entered the adjacent Darling Downs or ‘northern districts’ of NSW, accompanied by the assigned and ‘ticket-of-leave’ holders (convicts on probation). Moreton Bay, the Darling Downs, Stanley and the Burnett districts, became the southern areas of Queensland after 1859. Into the 1850s ‘ticket-of-leave’ holders and emancipists formed the basis of the labour force there.

 Table 8.1: Adult British Population in Pre-Separation Queensland, 1846 and 1851

 Writing of the late 1850s and the 1860s, A.C. Grant, the manager of Cadaga, a large sheep property in the Burnett district, remarked:

 A large number of the white men employed in those days were men who had been convicts and “Pentonville” exiles ... Having arrived in the district with the first white settlers, they seemed to attach themselves ... to the fortunes of their employers, apparently coming to the conclusion that they formed part and parcel of the station plant.[11]


-----

##### These ‘Pentonville exiles’, the last British convicts transported to the east coast of Australia, were despatched to pre-separation Queensland in 1849 and 1850. They were engaged by pastoralists, who continued to demand a labour force that was cheap and servile. In the late 1840s and early 1850s, masters on the Darling Downs might exploit the labour of assigned convicts, ‘ticket-of-leave’ holders, ‘exiles’ on probation, emancipists, Chinese, Indians, Germans and Aborigines.[12] in May 1844, two pastoralists, Philip Flierl and Gordon Sandeman, engaged twenty-five Indians from Calcutta despite the 1839 regulations prohibiting Indians from overseas agricultural and pastoral indenture. Two years later Flierl published a pamphlet entitled ‘The Advantages of Indian Labour in the Australian Colonies’ which unambiguously assured other potential importers that Indians were always ‘suspicious of kindness’.[13] His own policy was to intimidate, starve and flog his workers. These unfortunate servants took refuge with the Commissioner of Crown Lands, Dr Stephen Simpson, who refused to allow the Indians to return to work.[14] Subsequently, Flierl and Sandeman looked to China for indentured servants and in 1848, they introduced a further 108 Chinese who went on strike when they found their wages were far less than white workers performing the same tasks.[15] Over 2000 Chinese were introduced in the 1840s and 1850s altogether.[16]
 Following the classic patterns of colonial conquest and subjugation, the indigenous people were forced not only to cede their rights of ownership to the conquered territory but also to perform labour services for the new proprietor. These were exacted from them and were not subject to the contractual obligations of indentured servitude. Unlike the assigned convict who also worked without remuneration, the blacks were not being punished for designated crimes. They could not anticipate any remission of their ‘sentence’. The Aborigines were therefore de facto chattels bound securely to the new owners of their lands. In structural terms these sheep stations in nineteenth century Queensland possessed a quasi-slave mode of production, with the added complications of indentured service and wage labouring existing simultaneously. (See Ch.7)
 In the early 1860s, the distinguished ethnologist, Henry Maine, was selected by the Indian government to consolidate within a single framework all the legislation and regulations relating to Indian indenture overseas.[17] It was during this period of official reassessment that the newly-established colony of Queensland passed An Act to Give Force of Law to Regulations for


-----

##### the Introduction and Protection of Labourers from British India. It was enthusiastically sponsored by the governor, George Ferguson Bowen, a committed advocate of the utility and profitability of non-European servile labour for the tropical regions within the Empire.[18] His vision for Queensland duplicated and articulated those of the influential and often aristocratic pastoralists, and later sugar planters, who envisaged a society where they would reign supreme and unhindered over vast armies of servile non-white workers.
 The premises upon which their ideal was embedded were clear - Queensland possessed a sub-tropical physical environment where British labour seemed to them to be neither economically nor racially feasible. Therefore, in order to establish profitable industries, particularly sugar cane and cotton cultivation, it was deemed necessary to introduce the classical plantation system involving an exclusive wealthy elite (the proprietors), a numerically small technical and supervisory staff of Britons and thousands of legally servile ‘coolies’ to perform the arduous toil. Certainly this was one view, albeit a resoundingly persuasive one, in the new colony. The incipient urban liberal bourgeoisie who wanted a democratic white Australia opposed this. But their objections were not strong enough to prevent the introduction of non-European indentured servants.[19] It was not the outcome of political manoeuvres within the colony itself, but the Indian government’s delay in approving the 1862 Act which proved decisive. When it was eventually ratified in 1864, Queensland’s rural capitalists had already secured an extensive alternative source of labour and therefore no longer needed Indians. Melanesians from the Loyalty Islands and the New Hebrides were first introduced into Moreton Bay in August 1863 by the prominent entrepreneur Robert Towns. Their indenture had one outstanding advantage - Queensland would not be forced to compete with the established sugar colonies for Indian labourers. While the colony never enjoyed a monopoly in recruiting Pacific Islanders, it did provide the most attractive terms of service within the region.[20] Queensland sugar planters behaved more like their counterparts in British Guiana, for, though they might commit themselves primarily to one source of indentured worker, they were fully aware of the vagaries resulting from uncontrollable forces such as internal economic crises and slumps in the world sugar prices which crucially affected their operations. To this end, they experimented widely with various ethnic groups to meet the needs of changing economic circumstances, though the


-----

##### Melanesians existed as their mainstay from 1863 until 1904, when their further introduction was prohibited by the Commonwealth.
 In those years 62, 565 Pacific Islanders were introduced into Queensland, peaking numerically in the years 1881 to 1884 with a little over 14, 300. Over 65 per cent of all recruits came from the Loyalty, New Hebrides, Banks and Torres groups, though significantly over seventy per cent of all Solomon Islanders came in the period after 1890 (see appendix 1). This may be compared with the statistics for the Asians.

 Table 8.2: Ethnic Composition of Asian Workers in Queensland, 1871-1901.

 The tribal origin of the Melanesian population altered over time as did the age distribution and the relative proportions of ‘new chums’ and ‘old hands’. Peter Corris estimated that twenty-five per cent of Solomom Islanders in Queensland were young lads under the age of sixteen, the minimum which was laid down in the Polynesian Labourers Act of 1868. The majority were young men between the ages of sixteen and twenty-five. By the 1890s more than one quarter of recruits were ‘old chums’, a term used for returned Islanders who re-enlisted.[21] The Melanesian population in Queensland was always predominantly male, with females constituting less than ten per cent of servants. The Polynesian Labourers Act specifically required all Melanesian women recruits to be accompanied by their bona-fide husbands; though this was rarely the case as colonial servitude offered an escape for eloping unmarried couples. In April 1869, the Attorney-General informed the governor that ‘our policy should be so defined as to make ... [Melanesian] immigration a temporary aid to us and not to encourage its permanent settlement in this country’.[22] Two years later the Liberal parliamentarian John Douglas warned that:


-----

##### The existence of a servile class amongst us, protected by special legislation and unincorporable with the permanent population of the colony [the British] is radically opposed to the genus of Australian colonisation.[23]

 It was clearly envisaged that those Pacific Islanders who were brought to Queensland would not be allowed to become permanent settlers; for even if the institution of indenture was hoped by the pastoralists and sugar planters to be more than a temporary expedient there would be a constant turnover of individuals within it. Queensland wanted to avoid the mistake of the USA where Africans, introduced as a temporary measure in the 17th century, had become, by the end of the following century, permanent and entrenched chattels.[24] The policy by which young men enlisted without wives and children ensured that most remained in the colony only a few years. Moreover, the masters, always diligent in their efforts to maximise profits and minimise expenditure, were not therefore encumbered with the expense and responsibility of maintaining the unproductive units of small children and the elderly. It is ironical to contemplate that as far as efficient and experienced agricultural workers were concerned these would undoubtedly be women in Melanesia. Men would clear the tropical scrub and prepare the gardens for cultivation (as they were also required to do as indentured labourers in Queensland) but the routine and arduous work of tending the crops, weeding the gardens and harvesting was definitely ‘women’s work’. They were also the stock-keepers of domesticated pigs. Men in Melanesia, though this is a very broad generalisation, were engaged in house construction, fishing and canoe building, if ‘salt-water’ or coastal dwellers, and hunting if inland dwellers. Clearly, there existed a definite apportionment of labour on the basis of gender. Pacific Islander males entering into colonial servitude were invariably unaccustomed to routine and monotonous daily labour in the same way women were.
 The initial Islanders from Tanna, Lifu and Eromanga (all in the New Hebrides), engaged by the early cotton planters in the Nerang district of Moreton Bay, were employed in varying capacities, primarily as unskilled field workers. With more experience, some managed horse and bullock teams and others handled the innovatory steam ploughs.[25] Until the 1868 Act, when a term of three years’ servitude was laid down, most Islanders previously had been engaged for a term of one years’ indenture. Masters like


-----

##### Robert Towns rewarded those workers who decided to re-engage with increasingly skilled duties, though their remuneration was kept at £6 per annum. The labour crisis was more acute in the interior pastoral regions and by 1869, 697 of the 1539 Melanesians in the colony were located there,[26]
 employed predominantly as shepherds. The Rev. Frederick Richmond, an itinerant Anglican priest in the 1870s in western Queensland, caustically commented that:

 The kanakas [Melanesians] were employed to such an extent that the Queensland interior promised to become a wilderness of savages and dollar hunters. Stations [pastoral] as large as English counties would not employ more than half a dozen white men.[27]

 The wealthy pastoralist and former Indian planter, Robert Gray, recalled that:

 Owing to the difficulty of obtaining white labour, I had obtained a few South Sea Islanders, whom I had indented when down in Bowen. They were from Lifu and Tanna. During the time they were with me they were very useful and were fairly good at lambing and bush work.[28]

 Unlike the predominant system of gang labour on the coastal plantations, the exigencies of the ‘outback’ (as the dry interior of Australia is called) demanded small isolated groups. Charles Rome, the lessee of a large sheep station, reported that in order to prevent the Islanders ‘pining’ they would be ‘double-banked’,[29] so that two rather than one man would tend stock. The Melanesians hated working in the unfamiliar, desolate, dry sheep regions. The Rev. J.C. Kirby recalled seeing a group of Islanders travelling inland. This reminded him of ‘a scene from Uncle Tom’s Cabin, especially since their white guards on horseback were fully armed with pistols’.[30]
 As early as 1867, the Secretary of State for the Colonies wrote that he was dismayed that so many Melanesians were located on remote properties far from official supervision.[31] in 1877, the newly-elected Liberal government under John Douglas passed a Bill which confined first contract Islanders to sub-tropical and tropical agriculture, ‘as was intended’.[32] Though in some measure this may be attributed to humanitarian reformism, the primary reason lay with the Liberals’ desire to exclude Pacific Islanders and other imported non-Europeans from the colony. Certainly, when the conservatives


-----

##### (largely composed of pastoralists and planters) regained power in 1879, they did not reverse this decision. The following year they expanded upon the Liberals’ regulations. The reasons for this unusual unanimity are easy to detect. The conservatives recognised that the Pacific Islanders were no longer an essential component of the pastoral workforce; for with the increasing use of fencing and dams a large labour force was no longer necessary. Pastoralists, from the 1870s, began to rely upon white itinerant contract shearers rather than permanently employed shepherds.[33]
 Unlike the capital intensive pastoral station, the sugar plantation was both capital and labour intensive. Gradually all the available supply of imported indentured servants was withdrawn from the pastoral sector into tropical agriculture, which operated upon the basis of large numbers of unfree workers performing routine production tasks. Australia generally (and Queensland specifically) in the period 1860 to 1890 experienced an unprecedented expansion in production, volume of trade, and in flow of investment capital from Britain.[34] Paradoxically, the internal structure of the Queensland situation diverted it simultaneously into quite distinct and unique patterns within the Australian colonies, for no other colony possessed the physical environment and political conditions in which the classic plantation structure could emerge.
 The sugar industry underwent a series of changes and different labour patterns can be detected in each phase. The period 1862 to 1870 witnessed the ‘commitment to a staple’ phase. Sugar cane was initially cultivated in the more temperate southern coastal regions of the Logan and Moreton Bay, though by 1866 substantial plantations had been established in the more suitable tropical area of Mackay. The second major phase, the ‘Consolidation Phase’ from 1871 to 1879, was characterised by the rapid expansion northwards into the fertile areas of the Herbert, Johnstone River and Cairns districts. In these areas, planters would purchase large tracts of land and construct relatively primitive mills. Airdmillan Estate on the Lower Burdekin comprised 5000 acres and English speculators had invested £150, 000 by 1879.[35] During these years throughout the colony the acreage under cane expanded tenfold, the number of mills increased from thirty-nine to sixty- eight and the value of exports rose astronomically from £41 in 1871 to £275, 769 in 1879 (see appendix 3). This was consumed in the Australian market. The number of Melanesians being introduced into the colony rose from 643 in 1870 to 2182 nine years later (see appendix 1).


-----

##### These three characteristics - northern expansion, a classic plantation system and fluctuating prices and seasons - all developed in the ensuing period, the ‘Speculative Phase’, 1880 to 1885. These years were particularly crucial, for the plantation system matured, only to collapse spectacularly at the end of the decade. One third (18, 231) of the total number of Pacific Islanders who entered the colony from 1863 to 1904 arrived in these six years. This phase also witnessed unprecedented capital investment from Melbourne and London. Increasingly, though not exclusively, joint stock companies rather than wealthy, often aristocratic sugar planters owned sugar estates. As John Hinchcliffe had commented:

 The avocation of the sugar planters is certainly connected with success, wealth, intelligence, refinement and honour... in all countries [it] ... is the very highest in the whole scope of an agricultural profession.[36]

 The colonial planters strove to establish an aristocratic society with themselves at the apex, dominating their white and non-European workers under their leisurely paternalistic aegis. The sugar yield was very low, one and a half tons of sugar cane was crushed per acre.[37] As early as 1869, the planters were criticised for retaining inefficient and unproductive methods, such as employing vast numbers of servile workers toiling under the gang system ‘instead of improving cultivation methods’ and using heavy machinery and more sophisticated technology in the crushing and juice extractor mills.[38]
 The sugar planters and companies in the years of unprecedented expansion of the early 1880s, confident that the generous prices for sugar on the world market would remain stable, believed their most urgent problem was to secure an ever-increasing labour force of servile field-workers, rather than to improve productivity. But they were confronted with spiralling costs, for the cost of introducing each Melanesian recruit had increased from the usual £7 in 1863 to up to £16 in 1876 and £20 a few years later.[39] Concomitantly, the cost of returning labourers whose terms of servitude had expired, which legally had to be borne by the master, rose from £3.10.0 to £10 from 1863 to 1883.[40]
 The cost of wages for routine gang labourers was increasing too. The 1868 Act had stipulated that all first-contract workers should be paid £6 per annum with food, clothing, housing and medical attendance provided by the


-----

##### master. By the mid 1880s Melanesian field labourers on first-contract, given the intense demands for their labour, were able to command £15 p.a. whilst time-expired workers got £15 to £20 in the southern district of Bundaberg and up to £30 p.a. in the northern Herbert River area.[41] The Liberal parliamentarian, James Drake, was correct when he stated that ‘The Kanakas do not like field work anymore than white men’,[42] for a usual day meant labouring from 6am to 6pm with a brief breakfast and lunch break under extremely onerous and filthy conditions. They were constantly under guard by armed white overseers and their assistants, the gangers. The supervisory staff frequently used severe violence to force unwilling servants to toil or to maintain a faster pace as they weeded the large cane fields.[43]
 Increasingly from the ‘Consolidation Phase’ onwards, ‘time-expired’ workers (the contemporary term for Pacific Islanders who had completed their first three year term of indenture) chose other forms of labour rather than submit to the hated gang system. The conservative Legislative Councillor, A.J. Thynne, told his parliamentary colleagues in 1884 that he employed a ‘time-expired’ Melanesian as a sugar-boiler at £3 per week.[44]
 This was an unusual though by no means isolated case. Rather, most Islanders in this category would engage for short-term contracts as cane- carters, as unskilled or semi-skilled mill operators, or leave the plantation structure altogether and engage as casual labourers on farms and in the townships.
 In the years 1883 to 1884, attempts were made in several directions to ensure a continuance of reliable and cheap labour. First, labour recruiters were urged to be more diligent in the efforts to persuade Islanders to come to Queensland. The number of New Hebrides and Solomon Islanders rose from 1499 to 2542 and 61 to 1028 respectively in the years 1880 to 1883 (see appendix 1). Despite these substantial augmentations in the supply, this did not satisfy the demand. Recruiters went into the hitherto unexploited islands of New Britain, New Ireland, and the Louisiade and D’Entrecasteaux groups where the people had experienced only very sporadic contact with European traders and missionaries. In the years 1883 to 1885 nearly three thousand people from these areas were kidnapped, duped and treated with ferocious barbarity in order to augment the Queensland labour force.[45] Yet after ‘recruiters’ had fraudulently and violently obtained these ‘recruits’, they did not provide the solution to the industry’s labour shortage. Usually, newly- introduced recruits were ‘seasoned’. As Edward Denman of Etowi estate at


-----

##### Mackay explained: ‘for the first twelve months, the South Sea Islander is of very little use and requires great care and consideration.’[46] On large estates such as those owned by the conglomerate, the Colonial Sugar Refining Company (CSR), this procedure was invariably adhered to, but in the desperate demand for labour in these years many other planters directed their field staff to avoid this costly procedure. Subsequently, these Islanders died at the alarming rate of 147 per 1000 per annum.[47]
 Thirdly, the Pacific Island Labourers Act of 1880, which required that all first contract indentured servants be confined within tropical agriculture, was amended in 1884 providing three distinct labour categories. Those Melanesians who had been continuously resident in the colony since 1 September 1879 were given exemption tickets. Eight hundred and thirty-five were issued.[48] Ticket-holders were exempt from all restrictive provisions of the Pacific Island Labourers Act and could engage in any type of work, for how ever long they wished and at mutually agreed wages. All first-term workers were still required to serve an initial term of three years but ‘time- expired’ workers were not. They could choose new employers if they wished and bargain for shorter contracts. Most chose to work on six to twelve months contracts. But what was particularly significant was the stipulation that all Melanesians, except those with ‘tickets’, were henceforth legally obliged to perform only routine and menial labour in tropical agriculture. They were expressly forbidden to work as mill operatives, carriers, domestic servants and tradesmen. This legislation had been passed by the Liberal government, headed by Samuel Walker Griffith (who was later to draft the Commonwealth Constitution and become the first Chief Justice of the High Court). It was intended to segregate and contain most Pacific Islanders within one specified labour category in one industry. The Liberals’ motives were complex. First, they required the electoral support of recently enfranchised white working class men who feared competition with imported cheaper labour. Secondly, the Liberals were committed to the eventual establishment of a ‘White Australia’ and this was one step towards its realisation. They feared that a progressive more democratic society based upon a free British-derived population would be threatened and contaminated by all non-whites. Unwittingly, their racial ideology (which supported temporary segregation before virtual exclusion) allowed them to implement a policy which reinforced the very structures they strove to destroy. They were, in effect, ensuring that the sugar industry was not


-----

##### deprived of its labour force which would have been dispersed into more remunerative areas within the overall colonial economy. By designating that Islanders could only perform menial field tasks they were again reinforcing the hierarchical structure of the plantation by specifically outlawing upward socio-economic mobility. These non-Europeans were to be maintained legally in the rigorous caste-structure operating on the large estates. These two factors reinforced the economic ascendency of their political rivals, the conservatives, whose vision for Queensland was totally anti-democratic, for they wanted a small, wealthy coterie of British aristocrats and members of the bourgeosie to rule over vast armies of non-European indentured servants who were without any political and civil rights.
 Meanwhile, on the plantations, the proprietors in their haste to maximise profits and minimise costs now the Liberals had helped to stabilise their servile labour force, continued their cost minimisation strategies, such as providing the cheapest and unwholesome food (to such an extent that many Islanders spent most of their leisure time hunting birds and animals and fishing in order to survive); encouraging Melanesians to construct their own dwellings from cane sheaves and local unwanted material; to wear little clothing whilst working (though this could result in abrasions which would become easily infected in a tropical climate) and purchase their own clothes for their leisure time.[49] The planters were in a furore when the Liberals increased the capitation fee from 10/- to 60/- per Islander, in order to pay for the four government-run hospitals for Melanesians which were established in 1883.[50]
 The other main strategy which the planters employed was to secure alternative sources of labour. The Aborigines, as a hunting and gathering people, unlike the Melanesians from a peasant culture, did not possess the production and exchange relations or social organisation of even primitive capitalism. Structurally, they were not very effectively able to make the transition from hunter and forager to disciplined field labourer or wage labourer.[51] The Queensland frontier had been particularly brutal, so that in most instances, planters found it difficult to coerce surviving Aborigines to work. In the ecologically arid western areas of the colony, the Aborigines, deprived of their water supplies by the conquering pastoralists, had little alternative but to become herdsmen, herdswomen and domestic servants. Also, the more seasonal nature of a pastoral economy could be more readily adapted to the blacks’ former intricate nomadic patterns.[52] However, in the


-----

##### 1870s Aborigines from the Mackay Reserve had been used as an extra pool of labour, performing a variety of seasonally-determined tasks on the local plantations.[53] CSR’s extensive estates, Goondi and Mourilyan at Innisfail each employed fifty Aborigines.[54]
 As the Aborigines and Pacific Islanders could not provide the necessary number of labourers during the expansionist phase, the planters strove to find other suitable forms. In 1882 CSR’s Victoria estate on the Herbert River employed Chinese workers, though they were not allocated routine field production tasks but were made to construct drainage canals and tramway tracks. They were not cheap by any means, for most commanded 25/-per week plus rations, a common rate for white labourers.[55] Having mostly arrived as miners bound for the Palmer River diggings in the late 1870s and not specifically imported as indentured servants, they were not particularly amenable to plantation discipline. Moreover they could readily escape into the urban Chinese community. In 1880, 866 Javanese labourers arrived to work on CSR’s estates, but they were notably more expensive, at 6/9 per week than Islanders and they more frequently absconded.[56] In addition, Malays and ‘coolies’ from Singapore and Hong Kong were imported. Japanese were introduced primarily as domestics, laundrymen and skilled mill workers (especially centrifugal press operators).

 Table 8.3: Ethnic Origins of Labour Force in Queensland Sugar Industry in 1888


-----

##### The plantation structure, before its spectacular demise in the late 1880s and despite its manifold inefficiencies, comprised highly integrated systems. Each estate operated as a self-contained production unit, arranged along orderly, neat physical dimensions. Arthur Neame, who established Macknade which CSR later purchased, wrote that:

 I have laid out the plantation so that each block measures ten acres, the roads around each being 15ft. in width, a block on the river bank is reserved for the mill house, and most of the buildings for the men, the overseers and the staff, and cottages for the married people will be around this block.[57]

 The mill would invariably be placed near the river for easy and less expensive transportation. Buildings would usually be placed according to their relationship to production. Al Goondi these were constructed around a central parade ground where the overseers and gangers could muster the field-workers in a regimented manner, inspect them and give orders.[58] The government Inspectors of Pacific Islanders would conduct their twice yearly inspections when pay was distributed and complaints could be made.


-----

##### The whole operation was supervised by the manager, who might have two assistants. They would superintend both the operation of the field and the mill. One of the most important ‘officers’ (as CSR dubbed its supervisory staff) was the cultivation overseer who, as veteran West Indian planter and long-time resident in Queensland, John Buhot, remarked:

 On well ordered estates he places upon the manager’s table each morning an account of the work, not only of the gangs of labourers in his charge but of the work of individuals; lays out tasks and keeps a general account of the work done ... At 4pm he lays a detailed report again which ... are then taken to the bookkeeper, and by him the days work is credited and stoppages noted.[59]

 A well-organised plantation would be run along military lines, with its officers, NCOs and men representing a highly-defined class and caste system. Claudius Whish, granduncle of Australia’s first Governor-General, Lord Hopetown, who had seen active service in India, stated that he had found it an indispensable background for his later vocation as a planter in Queensland.[60] Orders were given and put into effect in a military manner, with parades, drill and vigorous supervision being an essential component of day-to-day life. The various ethnic groups lived in separate accommodation; the Melanesian usually in large barracks, for it was easy for the overseers to force them out in the morning for parade. The white staff were allocated houses according to their status, with the managers having well-appointed comfortable homes with Asian servants whilst white mill operatives would live in the European barracks (if single) and in a tiny cottage (if married).
 The various ethnic groups on the large estates would be designated specific occupational tasks and were also housed separately. Amongst the Melanesian community, Solomon Islanders would normally reside away from the New Hebrideans. Yet intertribal and inter-ethnic hostility was a daily occurrence. After serious inter-tribal fighting erupted on CSR’s Victoria estate in 1883, the company instructed its managers:

 ... to make it a rule not to allow the boys to take their tools into the houses, but to have a small shed erected specially for the purpose and provided with a lock and key.[61]


-----

##### In 1885 at Pioneer plantation on the Burdekin River a group of Malaitans and Chinese fought with mat-locks, tomahawks, bows and arrows and pick- handles. The field supervisor sent in a squad of Tannese to disrupt the brawl.[62] Clearly, these antagonisms deflected tension away from the white masters.
 In 1885 a series of complex political and economic factors converged in Queensland to promote profound structural changes in the sugar industry. In the previous year the price of sugar on the London market had plummeted to the unprecedented low of 13/3 per cwt.[63] During this decade the proportion of German and French beet sugar began to exceed the tropically-produced cane sugar. Distant Queensland was not immune from its effects. But during the optimistic and expansionist period of the early years of the decade, serious structural problems within the entire industry had been detected. The very detailed evidence presented to the 1889 Royal Commission to enquire into the depression in the industry demonstrated that far too much investment was channeled into the purchase and clearing of large tracts of virgin scrub rather than the acquisition of sophisticated technology and scientifically- trained staff.
 The Liberals in 1885 passed an Act to prohibit the further introduction of Pacific Islanders into the colony after 1890, which caused considerable apprehension amongst the already anxious planters whose efforts to retain ‘time-expired’ workers and secure Asian labourers had become all the more urgent. This government passed legislation which established two experimental central mills. Racecourse and North Eton at Mackay. Since 1876 the Land Act made the purchase of small selections more feasible, the number of modestly-endowed cane farmers had substantially increased. During the ‘Speculative Phase’, 1879-1885, many new farmers found themselves at the mercy of the planters upon whom they relied to crush their crop. Since cane needed to be harvested within twenty-four hours of cutting, a farmer needed to be certain his cane would be crushed that day. But since the planters had no need to compete with each other for the farmers’ patronage, each selector usually had to make the best arrangement he could with the nearest planter, who often refused to bind himself to a contract with farmers. As one selector told the 1889 Royal Commission:

 The central mill will give as much as can be given to the farmers who are growing cane for it. As it was before, the planters had it all in their hands.


-----

##### If I was a farmer close to the mill, I had to depend on the mill only and the planter gave me 7s. or 8s. or 9s. a ton for my cane and I had to sell it or let it stand.[64]

 The Liberal’s policy aided the development of an economic infrastructure which would allow yeomen farmers and their families to prosper. This legislation was unable to come to terms with the complexity of the inherent structural difficulties. It was the deepening depression in the industry, which heralded an intense Australia-wide depression, which changed the fundamental structure. As early as 1884, when sugar prices fell, Edward Long, the prominent conservative parliamentarian subdivided Habana estate at Mackay, so that he controlled only the milling of the cane and left the onerous responsibility of its production to small tenant farmers.[65] The powerful CSR followed suit, subdividing Homebush also at Mackay in 1891. By the early 1890s few plantations retained the structure of the buoyant expansionist era.
 The 1889 Royal Commission had endorsed this structural change, though there was basic disagreement between the commissioners on the question of coloured labour. Henry King and Alfred Cowley, both with long associations with sugar, argued that cheap, imported servile labour was absolutely necessary if the industry was to survive. The chairman, William Brookes, a staunch Liberal, issued a separate report recommending that in future only white labour be used. When the general depression strengthened, Griffith joined with his former political adversary, Sir Thomas McIlwraith, to form a coalition government. Increasing pressure from the northern separationists, mainly wealthy planters, could not be ignored, especially because of their influence in Britain, where they continued to press for a separate colony. Griffith issued his ‘Manifesto to the People of Queensland’ which contained proposals to reintroduce Melanesians for ten years. This dramatic volte face argued that this was merely a ‘temporary and transitional expedient’. Griffith added that he was still a determined opponent of non-European servile labour in the colony, but had been driven to this seemingly inconceivable move by the desperate times.[66]
 In 1893 The Sugar Works Guarantee Act was passed providing for the establishment of twelve more central mills which were initially financed by the government. These were far more efficient than the old plantation mills. In 1884-5 when there were 166 crushing mills, 368, 626 cwt of sugar was


-----

##### exported from the colony. Fifteen years later, when there were sixty-two mills, 2, 461, 150 cwt were exported (see appendix 3). In 1892 there were 450 sugar farmers in Queensland and five years later there were 1450. There was a proportional increase in the number of employers of Pacific Islanders, rising from 195 to 1264 in those years when Melanesian numbers fluctuated between 7489 and 8795.

 Table 8.4: Size of Farms and Plantations, Queensland 1900

 When the classic plantation system was in operation, Pacific Islanders were usually employed in gangs at the rate of one to four or five acres under cultivation.[68] Hambledon estate in Cairns, which had 900 acres planted, engaged 176 Melanesians, but this was a lower ratio than usual.[69] In comparison to the small farm/‘central’ mill system it was costly and inefficient. in this new system a ratio of only one worker per ten acres was required.[70] It would seem that Pacific Island labour was more experienced in


-----

##### sugar production by the late 1880s, as at least fifty percent were ‘time- expired’ workers (see appendix 1). In 1896, there were 763 introductions and 4773 re-engagements. Many were able to obtain 20/- to 25/- per week, a rate commensurate with that of white workers. With the transformation of the basic infrastructure, important changes can be detected in the patterns of labour. Although Islanders could earn higher wages, especially if they signed-on for a short-term contract with a new master, many were increasingly resentful. Most were legally still indentured servants. (Technically white and Asian workers were under the auspices of the Masters and Servants Act of 1861 as well.) Ackoa of Vanna Lava Island, speaking on behalf of many compatriots, asserted that:

 We want to be free labourers and do any work we can. We don’t want contracts. We say that as we do the same work that Hindoos (sic) and whitemen do, we should get the same wages as them.[71]

 Another Hebridean, Fred Tanna, stated that he worked for a Chinese farmer who did not demand a term of indenture and ‘plenty boy work like that’.[72]
 By the late 1880s and early 1890s, Pacific Islanders were becoming increasingly organised and assertive:

 They organise, keep up the rate of wages, have their alliances or unions, travel from place to place in search of better pay, communicate with each other by letter or wire.[73]

 At the same time, with the demise of the plantation system and gang labour, white workers felt it was no longer demeaning to their racial pride to engage as labourers in the cane fields. Formerly, any European who worked as a routine labourer in the industry alongside blacks or Asians was castigated as a ‘mean white’. With the depression in the pastoral and related transport industry from 1890, unemployed unskilled itinerant white people sought work in the sugar industry. CSR employed an experimental group of cane-cutters at Homebush in 1894 who were paid 3/6 per ton. They were not reliable workers as they frequently ‘knocked off’, and were replaced by Chinese labourers.[74] Dr Walter Maxwell, the Director of the Sugar Experimental Bureau calculated that 409 men were used to maintain only eighty-eight positions.[75]


-----

##### Gradually white workers began to organise their working procedures rather in the same manner as the ‘time-expired’ Melanesians had already done. The main organisation was called the ‘butty gang’ and it consisted of a number of cane cutters who would make a specific contract with a farmer to cut his cane at so much per ton. The team would share their earnings (along with the cook). The number in each gang decreased as workers obtained more skill at the arduous labour required to cut and trash cane. Initially, it would seem farmers would hire a team of twenty men in the early 1890s but this had decreased to twelve by 1906 and seven members six years later.[76] A farmer would normally retain around 20% of earnings until the contract was completed in order to ensure the members did not abscond. This system had very beneficial pecuniary effects as far as the employer was concerned. Unlike the old indenture system of Melanesians where masters had to pay recruiting costs, government capitation fees, as well as the expense of monitoring and supervising the daily output of servants in the field, the ‘butty gang’ system was internally controlled by its own members. It saved the employer money in two particular areas - he did not have to recruit or supervise this labour. If a team member of a ‘butty gang’ was unable to keep the pace of his fellows or was a ‘shirker’, he would be dismissed and replaced, a procedure that was not possible in the plantation gang system where the whip was the incentive to continue working at a specified pace.
 By the late 1890s political forces within Australia generally were gathering a momentum which would insist upon a ‘white’ continent, and in particular the expulsion of the Pacific Islanders from the Queensland sugar industry. As Robert Huttenback states:

 Of all the constituent parts of the British Empire, Australia ... seemed to make [itself a] ... bastion of Anglo-Saxon civilization preserved forever against the coloured races of the world.[77]

 The recently-established Commonwealth Government moved swiftly to introduce the Immigration Restriction Act (no.17 of 1901) where all intending immigrants were required to pass a written test of fifty words in English (and later any European language). This ‘Natal Formula’ had been in operation in some colonies since 1897. The Pacific Island Labourers Act of 1901 required that no more Melanesians enter Australia after 31 December 1904 and provided for their mass deportation in 1906 and 1907. This bill was passed without a division and as Peter Corris comments, ‘there were


-----

##### speeches made about it rather than debates upon it’.[78] Australian nationhood was to be accomplished by purging and expelling those people who, by their ethnic origins, were regarded as culturally, socially and biologically inferior to the dominant British.
 Although various humanitarian groups like the Aborigines Protection Society, some clergy and the various Pacific Islander organisations protested that the Commonwealth’s actions were illegal, improper and inhumane, the most vocal opposition emanated from the vested sugar interests. The Queensland premier, Sir Robert Philp, a former merchant with extensive interests in the sugar industry, continually reminded the Federal government that unless generous tariffs were implemented, sugar production would collapse without cheap black labour. Altogether, it was a rather spurious argument, for evidence already presented in this essay demonstrates that Melanesians by this time could generally earn wages comparable to white cane cutters. The Commonwealth passed the Excise Tariff Act of 1902 which placed an excise of £3 per ton on sugar grown by white labour until 1 January 1907. A protective tariff of £6 and £10 per ton was placed on foreign grown cane and beet sugar respectively. Cane growers who employed white cane cutters were awarded a bonus of 4/- to 5/- per ton in 1901. It was increased to 6/- to 7/6 per ton four years later.[79]
 Despite these provisions, Melanesians continued as the main source of cane cutters and sugar farm labourers. Many growers in the far north, hostile to the Commonwealth’s legislation, defiantly used Island labour exclusively. Statistics verify this pattern, for it is shown that most non-European labour was utilised in the far north.[80]

 Table 8.5: Proportion of Cane Harvested by White Labour in Queensland, 1904


-----

##### Delegates to the 1904 conference of cane farmers and millers declared that more financial incentives were necessary if white workers were to be attracted to the industry.[81] in 1901 there were 9324 Melanesians in Queensland, though by 1906 this had been decreased to 6389. A further 691 still held exemption tickets and a further 768 were aged between ten and fifteen and had presumably been born in Queensland. These persons as well as younger children born in Australia could not be deported. In 1906 the Queensland government, after a Royal Commission into the proposed deportation, recommended that the Federal authorities relax their strictures. In all 4269 persons were deported in 1906 and 1907.[82]
 Moves were also being made to improve the material conditions for white workers in the sugar industry. In 1905 the Shearers ’ and Sugar Workers ’ Accommodation Act was passed, proclaiming minimum sanitary and accommodation conditions. It was no coincidence that the years 1906 and 1907 witnessed the substantial transition from black to white labour in the cane fields. In 1906 the Sugar Workers Union was formed in Cairns and Mackay, for white workers only.[83] In 1906 white field workers in Bundaberg went on strike for better conditions.[84] That year the federal government increased the bounty from £3 to £4 a ton. In order to qualify, farmers had to pay labourers 22/6 plus ‘found’ per week in the off-season and 25/- in the harvesting period. Dr Maxwell estimated that wages of mill operatives rose by twenty-five per cent from 1905 to 1908.[85] In 1911 the white sugar workers (now in the Amalgamated Workers’ Association [AWA]) went on strike in all districts, except Maryborough, demanding an eight hour day and a minimum of 30/- per week. Companies such as CSR charged striking mill operatives with offences under the Masters and Servants Act. In a number of


-----

##### test cases before the Queensland Supreme Court, it was ruled that this Act no longer applied to white workers and was eventually rescinded by the newly elected Labor government in 1916.[86] White workers, whilst using this strike to illustrate their status as militant, organised unionists in contrast to supposedly unorganised dispirited indentured servants, also proclaimed their allegiance to a ‘White Australia’ policy and a ‘white’ sugar industry in particular. These mills were all owned by CSR, which continued to use non- European labour, a policy which had been abandoned in the state-financed central mills. CSR paid its Japanese centrifugal press operators at Goondi 34/4 per week and ‘found’ whilst Europeans got 30/-.[87] Clearly non- Europeans were not a form of cheap labour under-cutting white workers. In the 1911 confrontation Asians continued to work, thereby being castigated as ‘scab’ labourers. Unionists disregarded the fact that their union allowed only Europeans to join. The unionists’ antagonisms were racial, as Frederick Martin, an AWA secretary stated:

 These coloured people are unnecessary and a grave racial evil. They keep quarters which is [sic] ... a disgrace to any civilised community. I would not care how ... [the Japanese] are got rid of, so long as [they] ... are.[88]

 In August 1911 an agreement between the AWA and the Australian Sugar Producers’ Association was reached, giving mill workers a minimum wage of 30/- per forty-eight hour working week, but left field workers to make private agreements with farmers. Both groups were fiercely determined to impress upon both their employers and the public mind that they represented a ‘superior’ racial and industrial category. Their antagonisms to the newly- imported Italian workers who acted as a transitional category between servile non-white and militant white unionised labour was also apparent. The sugar workers’ refusal to accord non-British labourers the status of members of the working class like themselves betrayed a central weakness; it allowed more vulnerable groups of alien workers to be exploited by the employers without recourse to those sophisticated mechanisms of industrial relations, like the Conciliation and Arbitration Courts, which characterised the new Commonwealth.

 Table 8.6: Racial Composition of Selected Sugar Mills in Queensland in 1911


-----

##### Two years later, a series of Acts further diminished the position of non- white workers in the sugar industry. The Sugar Growers ’ Act of 1913 provided for the prompt payment to cane suppliers of a part of the value of the crop by millers. In the 1911 strike small-scale cane farmers had voiced their manifold grievances against both the private mills and many ‘central’ mills which were controlled by a small group of farmers to the detriment of their fellows. This Act sought to alleviate one major area of disharmony. Secondly, the Sugar Employees Act made temporary provision for wages and conditions for white workers, until these could be standardised by Awards under the regulations of the Industrial Peace Act of 1912.[89] But the most crucial legislation was contained in the Sugar Cultivation Act of 1913 which required all non-British aliens to pass a dictation test in any language as the Secretary for Agriculture might decide. As Easterby stated:

 The object of this Act was to shut out certain coloured labour or aliens as might be required, or to prevent such persons from owning or cultivating


-----

##### land for sugar cane - all this in furtherance of the growing of cane by white labour.[90]

 Though the Commonwealth legislation provided for a dictation test for any intending immigrant into Australia, only Queensland maintained legislation with similar provisions and methods which related only to internal domestic issues. Other subsequent legislation like the Banana Industry Preservation Act of 1913 further restricted the right of the non-British to work in tropical agriculture. The Agricultural Bank Act Amendment Act of 1904 specified that aliens were not eligible for loans whilst the Lands Act of 1910 and the Leases to Aliens Restriction Act of 1911 curtailed the rights of Asians and Pacific Islanders to become leaseholders. Persons of non-European descent were further restricted in employment under the Dairy Produce Act 1904-1920, the Margarine Act of 1910-1930, the Queensland Local Authority Act 1902- 1920 (railway, bus and tramway employment) whilst the Queensland Mining Act 1899-1930 forbade unnaturalised non-whites from obtaining mining leases. The Sugar Cultivation Act required aliens who wanted exemptions to apply before the end of 1913. There were subsequently many cases of extreme hardship, for no more tickets were issued after 1914 and many people, not understanding the bureaucratic procedures, did not correctly apply. If found working in the industry without a ticket they were fined by the police or the Department of Agriculture. The Labor government which came to power in 1915 was adamant in its refusal to grant more exemptions. In all, 1654 exemptions were issued.[91] (See Table 8.7). This means that most aliens who applied for an exemption as required under the Act were granted it, for the 1911 Census of the Commonwealth which gave the most accurate figures revealed there were 1735 Asians and Islanders in the sugar districts. (See Table 8.8).
 Despite the severity of those Acts, the unions, in particular the powerful AWU, were still campaigning throughout the war years to expel all Asians from Queensland. Strikes were frequently threatened ‘if coloured aliens are not removed at once’.[92] With the intense ideological and class conflict of the war years escalating after 1918,[93] the sugar industry was to prove a significant industrial battleground; but rather than pursue direct action against monopolies like CSR, the AWU continued to deflect attention away from class issues and reassert their traditional preoccupation with racial


-----

##### exclusiveness and xenophobia. Thomas Quilty, the AWU organiser at Cairns, wrote to the Minister for Agriculture in February 1919 stating that:

 I have received numerous complaints and the menace seems to becoming greater every year and areas that were practically all white previously are now employing coloured aliens. Hambledon, Babinda and the Innisfail areas have always been overrun with Asiatic labour, but Mulgrave was always a mill that employed all whites; but now there are indications that it is going to be engulfed in coloured ocean like the others. The situation is a very serious one for us, and unless something is done, it will only be a matter of time till the sugar industry is a coloured one again.[94]

 The Permanent Under Secretary of the Agriculture Department replied that the Minister ‘had every sympathy with your point of view’.

 Table 8.7: Occupation of Exemption Holders under Sugar Cane Cultivation Act of 1913


-----

##### Table 8.8: Ethnic Groups in Each Sugar District, Queensland 1911


-----

##### The unions considered it necessary, especially ‘in view of the sacrifices of Australian soldiers’, which supposedly intensified antagonisms against the non-British segment of the population, to fight even more vigilantly to obtain a ‘white’Queensland.[95] The Indian Overseas Association protested to the Prime Minister in August 1920 that, in view of the Indians’ contribution to the war effort, it was regrettable that these British subjects were discriminated against in the Queensland sugar industry. They resented the


-----

##### concession given to the non-British Japanese primarily for reasons of international diplomacy.[96] The AWU began a campaign of harrassment against non-British aliens and their employers. William Storrie, the organiser at Ayr, was concerned that twenty Japanese men were working at Inkerman mill and on the tramways. It was an affront with so many ‘unemployed looking for work’. Though frequently the unions and the returned servicemen’s organisation were enqaged in bitter ideological conflict, in this instance there was harmony and agreement that Queensland was the home of the British. Storrie continued that the RSL (the Returned Servicemen’s League) ‘wants something done’.[97]
 The union organisers would make surprise visits to farmers suspected of employing Asians or Islanders without exemption tickets. The Innisfail official, Quilty, must have been particularly vigilant and increased the police workload; for when he reported, in October 1917, a qang labouring outside the award hours, the local sergeant simply refused to investigate.[98] As well as this, the AWU pressed the local inspectors of Factories and Shops, who were to superintend the regulations of the Sugar Cultivation Act of 1913, to be particularly zealous in detecting illegal labour. The Chief Inspector in Brisbane wrote that his officers could not cope with all the AWU complaints and suggested the police give more assistance.[99] When such complaints were made, a prosecution was often difficult. William Watt, the energetic and often overworked inspector at Innisfail reported that he.came across two Malays in a canefield who ‘denied working at all despite the firmly chipped ground’. The most implausible stories were offered explaining their presence in the canefields.[100] The other difficulty involved the non-cooperation of employers. As the Under Chief Secretary reported:

 It is thouqht that in some cases, coloured men have a secret agreement with their employers which enables them to work at less rates than those prescribed by the sugar industry awards.[101]

 Some farmers were very sympathetic to the plight of Pacific Islanders without exemption tickets who could not perform their accustomed duties cultivating and cutting cane. Fearful of incurring the emnity of the unions and fines by the Factory Inspectors, some farmers engaged Melanesians to work at night. Noel Fatnowa, a descendant of the Malaitans at Mackay, said his parents would leave their children and venture to the silent canefields and


-----

##### aided only by the light of the stars and the moon, they would stealthily strip, trash and cut the cane.[102]
 The Queensland sugar industry by the 1920s had undergone extraordinary transformations and was virtually organised on the principle of white labour only, thereby rendering it unique. This dedication to an organised, militant white workforce was all the more unusual in that only eighty years previously Australia possessed an extensive form of quasi-slavery and forty years later, Queensland still operated a fully matured classical plantation system. Unlike other sugar producing colonies in the British Empire, Queensland was a ‘settler society’, dominated by a British derived population and British social institutions. The commitment of the Commonwealth of Australia to a ‘White Australia’ policy in 1901 further accelerated the process of structural change in the sugar industry by outlawing the expansion of Melanesian indenture and gradually introducing a viable white labour force.
