#### Chapter Nine

 CHINESE INDENTURED LABOUR IN THE TRANSVAAL GOLD MINING INDUSTRY, 1904-1910

 Peter Richardson

##### Asia has been a source of labour supply for South Africa since the seventeenth century. Within a few years of the Dutch settlement of the Cape, slaves were imported from the Malayan archipelago to satisfy demand for continous labour which could not be supplied by the indigenous African population. For close on one hundred and fifty years the Cape ‘Malay’ population formed the only Asian element within the Dutch settlement area.[1] In the mid-nineteenth century, however, with the extension of European colonisation beyond the Orange and the Vaal rivers and across the Drakensburg mountains into present-day Natal, the demand for a regular labouring population to work on European-owned land turned attention once again to the labour reservoirs of the Asian continent.
 In line with other tropical-produce colonies in the mid-nineteenth century, Natal looked not to the south-eastern areas of Asia, but to India. Early in the 1850s, a deputation of coast planters sought government help in arranging for the introduction of Indian indentured labourers to meet the growing shortage of labour in the sub-tropical coastal districts.[2] After fairly protracted negotiations, Indian government sanction was obtained, and importation began in 1860. This continued until 1866 when a severe depression in the Colony led the planters to request the cessation of the trade. This was resumed in 1874 with the return of prosperity and continued, with only a short break in 1886, until 1911, when the Government of India refused to permit any further indentured emigration to Natal.[3]
 Like the Cape, the colony of Natal had sought labour from Asia on conditions of service that would provide the settlers with sufficient cheap unskilled labour on a regular basis to meet the need created by commercial cultivation of tropical crops. Cash cropping in these areas was particularly


-----

##### concerned with sugar production, although tea was a significant crop from the 1870s onwards.[4] The development of the Natal midlands was also associated with a growing demand for Indian labour, although on a much smaller scale. The surge of growth in the two maritime colonies of the Cape and Natal which followed the discovery of diamonds in Griqualand West, resulted in further demand for Indian labour, this time to construct railways, harbours and other public works. Consequently, from the 1870s the demand came increasingly from industrial enterprises associated with this expansion, rather than from the agricultural sector as hitherto. In the 1880s, the discovery of large coal reserves in the northern areas of Natal further emphasised this changing pattern of demand. With the growing use of coal as fuel to drive the steam machinery on the mines of Kimberley and, after 1886, the Witwatersrand, the Natal collieries found a ready and expanding market for their produce.[5] The direct association of indentured labour with the burgeoning mining industry of South Africa was significantly enlarged by the exploitation of extensive gold deposits of payable ore on the Witwatersrand in the Transvaal after 1886. Within thirteen years of the declaration of the Witwatersrand as a public digging, the South African Republic had become the largest single source of gold production in the world. By the time of Union (1910), no less than 35 per cent of all new gold came from this one source. A development of this magnitude was based on the enormous extent of the gold deposits of the Witwatersrand and upon the conquest of the technological problems of recovering gold from pyritic or refractory ores, most strikingly illustrated in the widespread use of the Macarthur-Forrest cyanide process after 1891. This latter development, and the extensive capital investment in sophisticated machinery which accompanied it, led eventually to the economic exploitation of lower grade deposits, and made possible the extension of payable ground along the East and West Rand, beyond the rich central section around Johannesburg.[6]
 The scale of mining which derived from these processes can be deduced from bare statistics of its operations. In 1887 total Transvaal output of gold was 39, 880 fine ounces worth £169, 401. By 1899 this figure had increased to 3, 637, 713 fine ounces at a value of £15, 452, 025. By 1909 this figure had doubled to 7, 295, 108 fine ounces, worth £30, 987, 656. Similarly, the number of African miners employed increased from 14, 000 in 1887 to 99, 000 in 1899 and to 155, 000 in 1909. In 1907, the total output of 6.4 million fine ounces of gold required the milling of 25.6 million tons of gold ore.


-----

##### Not surprisingly, the amount of capital invested, which was primarily controlled by large finance houses or Groups, was very great - by 1900 £77.4 million at par had been subscribed, a figure which had increased to £121.4 million by 1910. Between 1886 and 1910 not less than £77.2 million had been distributed in dividends.[7]
 The regional impact of this development was such that recruiting agents were soon scouring the whole of the southern African sub-continent in an attempt to meet the demand for cheap unskilled mine labour. One solution to this problem was found in the indenturing of very large numbers of African miners from the southern provinces of Portuguese East Africa. The populous areas of Gaza, Inhambane, and Lourenco Marques south of Lat.22° South, provided the mining industry with its largest single source of long-contract labour in the period 1886-1914. At the time of Union, half of the industry’s African labour force was made up of so-called ‘East Coast Natives’.[8] Nevertheless, this successful utilisation of indenture to provide a stabilising element to the mine workforce only partially met the labour demands of the industry. The volume of production in the gold mining industry, the development of competitive demands for labour from European agriculture and the continuing vitality of African production, ensured that labour shortage was virtually endemic in the mining industry.
 This situation was transformed by the outbreak of the Anglo-Boer War of 1899-1902. As a result of the conflict, a labour force which had been barely adequate, was dispersed as mines were forced to close for the duration. At the same time, the mineowners compounded their own difficulties by announcing a wage reduction in 1900 to become effective once the war had ceased. This was to be implemented by the newly formed Witwatersrand Native Labour Association [WNLA]. The effects of this development were soon felt in the immediate post-war period as a large amount of labour was required to undertake reconstruction and to expand infrastructural development planned by the newly installed British administration.[9] Such increased demand occurred at a time of diminished supply largely as a result of a series of good harvests in African areas and high prices emanating from military needs. Nor could the mineowners strengthen their position by relying on the state to force out African labour. Such an apparatus simply did not exist in the immediate aftermath of the war, and was furthermore politically unacceptable because of the likelihood of it provoking an African rising.[10]


-----

##### The resultant shortage of labour in the gold mining industry led ultimately to the decision to turn once again to Asia, this time to import Chinese miners on three year indentures. Such an outcome has been taken as indicative of a labour crisis in the Transvaal at this time. This view, shared by most contemporaries, has been endorsed frequently by historians.[11] Yet an examination of events leading to the importation of the Chinese suggests that labour shortage, although real enough, was only part of a more fundamental crisis of accumulation in the industry, associated with an enforced transition to lower grade deep level mining. Another manifestation of this was a long term fall in the rate of profit. As this is not a generally accepted view of the so-called Reconstruction Period (1901-6), but is central to an understanding of the role of Chinese labour in the mining industry, it is now necessary to examine the claim more closely.
 The mining industry’s response to the labour shortage must be sought not only in the dispersal of the mine labour force by the authorities of the South African Republic in 1899, and in the decision by WNLA to reduce African wages, but also in the effects of the War itself upon the capital structure of the mining industry. With the cessation of mining in 1899, output plummeted and dividends virtually ceased. Appropriation of surplus revenue for internally generated investment likewise slumped, whilst debts and interest payments accumulated and payment on debentures fell into arrears, stocks of gold were exhausted and overheads increased. In financial terms the industry estimated it had incurred a direct loss of £6.9 million, and an indirect loss of £25 million as a result of the War.[12] Nevertheless, investment in the form of new working capital continued to be subscribed in London, Paris and Berlin. Much of this capital was speculative, encouraged by the prospect of a British government in the Transvaal, and was ear-marked for new or developing companies. There were two phases to this inflow. In 1899, in anticipation of a quick British victory, funds came in in quite substantial amounts. These dried up when it became clear that the struggle was going to be prolonged. However, in 1902, after the signing of the Peace of Vereeniging, the process began again. In the short lived boom which followed, some £10.2 million was brought into the industry as working capital, and a further £5.5 million as vendors’ interests. In 1902 alone no fewer than 299 new companies were floated.[13]
 The intensifying demands for the profitable employment of this new capital and attempts to recoup some of the war-time losses argued for a


-----

##### rapid and extensive restarting of mining operations. The difficulty was that in the conditions of the war-torn Transvaal, the means were not immediately available. However, the pressures operating on the mining companies were such that the condition of the country did not stop them attempting to implement this policy. This can be seen in a series of crucial decisions which were undertaken at this time. Thus, the attempt by WNLA to reduce wages not only held out the prospect of an immediate reduction in working costs, but implied an extensive labour supply at reduced rates which encouraged much new investment. The companies built on this situation in May 1901, when, with the limited restarting of mining operations, they decided to distribute all available labour to the widest range of producing companies. This policy was continued until November 1903, when automatic preference for individual crushing companies was suspended and distribution given over to the parent Groups. An essential underpinning of this strategy was preference for Transvaal coal companies which were members of WNLA, to enable them to supply fuel to the gold mines.[14]
 In response to these initiatives, in a period of eight months between December 1901 and July 1902, thirty-five companies restarted mining operations, 1, 632 stamps came into operation and the monthly output of gold increased by 182 per cent.[15] At the same time, companies curtailed sorting of waste rock to economise on labour and introduced a sizeable number of large-machine drills, often operated by more expensive unskilled white labour. The net result was a dramatic fall in working profits, for the intermix of waste rock was increased by the suspension of sorting, and a larger amount of non-ore bearing rock was mined by use of machine drills. Between December 1901 and 1902, the grade of ore treated fell by 7s.3d per ton whilst costs fell much less sharply.[16]
 This development would not necessarily have been serious if it had been accompanied by a commensurate fall in the wage element of working costs or a sharp rise in the purchasing power of gold. The reverse of these two things happened. African wage rates rose constantly under the pressure of competitive demand from within the industry and from other sources, whilst no cheaper alternative form of unskilled labour was immediately available. Thus between 1901-2 and 1903-4, the average monthly wage paid to African miners rose from 26s.8d to 48s.10d.[17] At the same time the purchasing power of gold fell to an all-time low since mining operations had commenced on the Witwatersrand. In 1902-3, the value of gold was


-----

##### thirty-eight index points below its immediate pre-War level. Despite a slight rise thereafter, this was never sufficient to offset a rise in working costs emanating from escalating food and wage costs.[18]
 As early as mid-1902 the effects of this profit squeeze could no longer be ignored. In August 1902, the major mining groups employed only 23.2 per cent of their complements, with grades falling and costs rising.[19] In consequence, the Chamber of Mines decided to appoint a sub-Committee on Labour to investigate ways in which the supply could be augmented, a move which initiated official consideration of the possibility of importing Chinese labour. At this time Chinese importation was only considered as a last resort.[20] However, between mid-1902 and 1903, the continuation of the pressures on profitability outlined above, and the emergence of new factors, forced the industry into a policy of wholehearted support for immediate Chinese importation.
 Between the appointment of the Chamber’s own sub-Committee on Labour in 1902 and the first meeting of the Transvaal Labour Commission in July 1903, two further influences were brought to bear. First, the stock market boom in gold shares began to evaporate in the wake of increasing evidence of the serious situation in southern Africa. The collapse had very important consequences. At a time when working profits were falling, it added greatly to the pressure on available funds to generate new working capital. As a result, dividends as a proportion of gross yields from mining operations started to fall persistently. On the other hand, the necessity of continuing to distribute dividends to maintain market confidence and hold up share values led to a marked deterioration in the value of ore reserves as mine managers struggled to work the higher grade ore bodies to off-set the poorer returns from un-sorted machine-drilled ore. Faced with this situation, the mines attempted to spread the burden of rising costs over greater reserves, and began, in 1904, to raise their stamping capacity. The result was an accelerated and enforced transition to lower grade mining, accompanied by a marked deterioration in yield and profit because of the companies’ inability to reduce working costs rapidly.[21] Between 1904 and 1910, the average grade of ore mined fell by a further 27 per cent, whilst the net return on capital fell from 8.5 per cent to 2.6 per cent.[22]
 The industry was also forced to support the importation of Chinese labour by developments within the labour market for Portuguese East


-----

##### Africa, hitherto the staple supply of indentured labour for the mines. As in other areas, such as mineral taxation and tariff policy, the British administration of the Transvaal had from an early date recognized the importance of this labour market bv negotiating a modus vivendi with the Portuguese authorities on labour recruiting and railway policy in 1901.[23]
 Despite this formal agreement, labour supplies, here as elsewhere, were subject to fluctuations. In the first two years of resumed operations, the rate of supply was steady and considerable numbers came forward - 38, 631 in 1902 and 43, 625 in 1903. However, towards the end of 1903 indications from recruiters in the field showed that increases of this order could not be sustained, even from the most populous regions. In 1904 these fears were realised when only 27, 633 men were recruited by WNLA south of Lat.22° South.[24] The over-dependence of the industry on this source of indentured labour, which this situation revealed, was readily appreciated by its leaders who saw Chinese labour as a means of avoiding potentially serious embarrassment following restrictive or retaliatory action from the Portuguese authorities.[25]
 The political situation in the Transvaal and Britain also dictated the pattern and pace of events leading up to the introduction of Chinese labour. Thus, the growing body of favourable opinion within the industry was kept largely secret until March 1903 when Sir George Farrar, chairman of the Anglo-French Group of mining companies, opened the public campaign in support of importation.[26] Prior to that, the Chamber’s sub-committee on labour had unanimously recommended in December 1902 that investigations be made into the labour potential of the Far East, a decision which bore fruit in the despatch of H. Ross Skinner and Herbert Noyes to China on a fact-finding mission in February 1903.[27] By the time Skinner and Noyes returned to South Africa in September to report their findings that Chinese labour was an economic possibility for the industry, the government of the Transvaal had also come out publicly in favour of importation. At the same time it had appointed the Transvaal Labour Commission to investigate the causes of the shortage and recommend a solution. With the industry’s virtual monopoly of the media, favourable official opinion and growing depression in the country, the result of the deliberations of the Commission was a foregone conclusion. When its majority report was published in November 1903, its recommendations


-----

##### were taken as thinly veiled support for the Chinese.[28] This was confirmed by a vote in the Chamber of Mines in early December 1903, which requested government sanction to the immediate introduction of an ordinance permitting importation.[29] This was accepted, and at the end of 1903 it introduced a draft measure into the Legislative Council of the Transvaal. This became law in February 1904, subject to the sanction of the Imperial Government. This was given on 11 March 1904.[30] The successful negotiation of an international Convention between Britain and China on behalf of the Transvaal government in May 1904 completed the legal apparatus on which the introduction of Chinese labour was based.[31]
 The historical significance of the introduction of indentured Chinese miners was not confined to southern Africa. The whole episode, from its beginnings in 1902-4, through to the wholesale repatriation of the Chinese work force of 53, 828 men between 1907 and 1910, was also an important chapter in the history of Chinese indentured emigration. The negotiation of the Anglo-Chinese Labour Convention of 1904, and the subsequent recruiting in north and south China between 1904 and 1906, was the first example of Chinese indentured emigration under mutually recognised government regulation. It was also the first example of this type of emigration on any scale and under any form of supervision since the closure of the Macao ‘coolie’ trade in 1874.[32] In its organisational technique and expertise it was clearly distinct from its nineteenth-century predecessors, providing a blue-print for the larger utilisation of Chinese labour by the French and the British Armies in the First World War.[33] Furthermore, in its predominantly northern Chinese focus, the migration to the Transvaal underlined trends in population movements of twentieth-century China, rather than continuing those of the nineteenth century, which had been largely southern in emphasis. Even the correlation of Chinese migration and gold mining was significantly different in this case, for whereas Australian and United States Chinese had gone under varying forms of bondage, they rarely sailed as genuinely indentured emigrants as did the Chinese going to the Rand.[34]
 Some form of state regulation was bound to be imposed upon the Transvaal trade. Ever since 1855, with the enactment of the Chinese Passenger Act, varying degrees of government control had been exercised over Chinese emigration by British authorities. This was in spite of the fact


-----

##### that the 1866 and 1868 regulations to the Convention of Peking of 1860 had been rejected by one party or the other.[35] Nevertheless, in 1889, in the wake of the fast developing steam passenger traffic in the China trade, Hong Kong Ordinance 1 further extended government regulation over British ships conveying Chinese emigrants from Chinese ports, and over all ships conveying Chinese emigrants from Hong Kong. Although this process was less extensive than the legal superstructure which governed Indian indentured emigration to British and other colonies at the time, by the late nineteenth century, the Chinese passenger trade was far from being unregulated.[36]
 The particular pattern of state intervention in the Transvaal trade was determined by a combination of forces emanating from the conditions governing Chinese emigration at the time the Transvaal mine owners sought access to the Chinese labour market, and from considerations acting in the Transvaal and Britain. Briefly, in the Transvaal itself, the conflicts which emerged within the white polity, particularly between the white working class, the white traders and the agricultural interests and the mine owners over labour and possible competition from an influx of Asian traders, was such that the mining industry actively lobbied for state support to control these conflicts. At the same time, the industry appreciated that conflicts between the Chinese workforce and the mine owners themselves also reauired state intervention.[37] In Britain the affront given to humanitarian and non-conformist opinion by the proposal to introduce Chinese into the Transvaal, was matched by the opposition from organised labour. Both of these groups were powerful enough in Parliament to force the British government to impose certain controls upon the conditions of importation and service in the Transvaal.[38] In China, the recognition that some form of emigration to the Transvaal could not, under the terms of the Convention of Peking, be refused, was accompanied by a determination to ensure some form of metropolitan control over western colonial labour practices.[39]
 The result was a complex legal importation structure. Two major pieces of legislation governed the operation of the traffic. The basic legal document was the Anglo-Chinese Labour Convention of May 1904, which was designed to replace the unratified Regulations to the Convention of Peking of 1860. The 1904 Convention laid down the broad conditions of government regulation in respect of recruiting, shipping and conditions of


-----

##### contract of service for British colonies as a whole. This document is of particular interest in that it combined regulations governing the Indian and Chinese emigrant trades to British colonies. This can be most clearly seen in the schedule to the Convention which set out particulars in relation to shipping space and provisions which were directly determined not by the Chinese Passenger Acts, but by the Indian Emigration Acts of 1883 and after.[40]
 Specific sanction to import labour under the terms of the Anglo-Chinese Labour Convention was provided by the Transvaal Labour Importation Ordinance of 1904, together with regulations issued under authority of section 29 of that Ordinance.[41] Generally speaking, the Ordinance and Regulations elaborated six major provisions of the 1904 Convention. These were: the right of a British colony to import labour from China, the obligation to appoint a protector of Chinese labour to oversee conditions of employment (termed the Superintendent of Foreign Labour together with a subordinate Foreign Labour Department [FLD]), the right to free repatriation and the right of free emigration for labourer’s families, prohibition on the speculative transfer of labourers, standardisation of a general form of indenture of service, together with a guaranteed minimum wage, and lastly, the control of conditions of embarkation and shipping. Subsequently these provisions were elaborated further by the issue of Instructions to Transvaal Emigration Agents in China and to the Inspectors of Chinese Labour placed along the Rand.[42]
 The FLD became the means by which all the obligations and functions of the Transvaal and the British governments were coordinated. To effect this role, the FLD combined both inspectoral and executive functions. Thus in the Transvaal, at the mines, and in Natal at the port of Durban, the Department had an inspectoral role. This was changed when a few judicial powers of fining were added to the powers of the mine inspectors under the terms of the Labour Importation Amendment Act of 1905.[43] In China, the FLD had not only an inspectoral role to play over the whole of the embarkation procedure, but also acquired through its Transvaal Emigration Agents, an executive position by virtue of its right to control the receiving depots at the coast ports. This dual position originated in the desire of the Superintendent of the FLD to control the activities of the recruiting agents by limiting access to the recruits on arrival at the port.[44] These different


-----

##### functions of government, described by the mining industry as ‘a confusion of the functions of Government and private enterprise’, were a direct result of obligations undertaken in London and South Africa.[45]
 The consequence of these arrangements was paradoxical. On the one hand, a high degree of state intervention was required to make importation a practical, legal and political possibility. On the other hand, such arrangements also raised the unit cost of importation and employment, and threatened to restrict the usefulness of Chinese labour as a general panacea for the ills of the mining industry. To resolve these contradictions, the mining industry, acting in unison, devised a variety of policies which covered the whole range of cost problems associated with the introduction and utilisation of this type of indentured labour. Recruiting, shipping and employment practices were affected to such an extent that the use of Chinese labour in the Transvaal gold mining industry, albeit for only six years, marked a major development in the history of the use of indentured labour within the British Empire.
 The basis of the mining industry’s control over recruiting and shipping was established at an early stage. Thus, from October 1903, finance and control of the operation in China was in the hands of a Committee of Agents representing the whole of the mining industry of the Transvaal.[46]
 Subsequently, when introduction had been guaranteed by state intervention this committee was dissolved and replaced by a more permanent organisation known as the Chamber of Mines Labour Importation Agency
 [CMLIA]. This was a non-profit-making limited company registered in the Transvaal on 15 July 1904.[47] This agency was closely modelled on WNLA, with which it ultimately merged during the repatriation of the labourers in 1908.[48] Through this institution, the cost constraints of Rand gold mining were brought to bear directly upon the recruiting and shipping arrangements.
 Control over these central aspects of the importation procedure was achieved in two ways. First, the CMLIA acted as sole importer for the industry, thereby conferring the advantages of a highly capitalised, non- competitive buyer upon the individual mining companies who contracted with the Agency. Second, the vertical integration of managerial power within the company, ensured rapid and effective response to initiatives undertaken by the industry at any point in the importation procedure. These aspects of the CMLIA rationalised investment, reduced risk and unit cost,


-----

##### and at the same time ensured access to state power and the widest possible exploitation of the Chinese labour market. This latter feature ensured the maximising of the potential labour supply at the same time as it insured the industry against the possibility of recruiting failures in any one part of the country.[49]
 It is important to appreciate that the CMLIA was not a recruiting or shipping company in the strict sense of these terms, for in both aspects of the importation process, it acted as a contractor or middle-man for the individual companies. Thus, in south China where the experiment began in February 1904, the agency contracted with the British firm of Butterfield and Swire at Hong Kong to provide them with recruits, ships and provisions for the trip to Durban. Swire’s agency was deemed to include all of south- eastern China as far north as the treaty port of Swatow. The central provinces were divided for recruiting purposes between Jardine, Matheson & Co., and Gibb, Livingstone & Co. For the northern provinces, four firms were chosen, two based on the Shantung port of Chefoo, two based on the Chihli port of Tientsin. At Chefoo, the agency was divided between Cornabe, Eckford & Co. and Silas Schwabe & Co., and at Tientsin between William Forbes & Co. and the Chinese Engineering and Mining Co. Ltd. Like the recruiting contracts, shipping arrangements were initially made with western firms: Butterfield and Swire at Hong Kong in the south, and Livingston, Halton & Co., chartering brokers, in the north.[50] Throughout the period of active recruiting from February 1904 to November 1906, therefore, the South African experiment relied upon the apparatus of the Treaty Port system for the exploitation of Chinese labour resources.[51]
 By and large, despite this welter of contractual relations, the CMLIA was able to dictate successfully the pattern and pace of recruiting and shipping. Thus, between February 1904 and January 1907, 63, 695 Chinese labourers were landed in South Africa. The shipments were distributed effectively to meet peak demand from the mining companies. Despite an annual loss of labour power through death disease and repatriation, varying between 6.6 per cent in 1905 and 15.5 per cent in 1907 of those employed, the number landed represented an effective addition of 53, 828 men to the unskilled labour force of the Transvaal mines at the height of the experiment in January 1907.[52] Between 1906 and 1907, Chinese labourers represented


-----

##### over 35 per cent of the total unskilled work force on the mines, the largest single group of indentured labourers at that time.[53]
 The great majority of these men were recruited from the northern provinces of Shantung and Chihli. No less than 62, 006, or 97.3 per cent of the men embarked for the Transvaal mines came from the northern ports and of these approximately 93 per cent were from these two most northerly provinces.[54] Such a pattern of recruiting was not anticipated at the beginning of importation. It had originally been the industry’s intention to exploit the more familiar emigration grounds of Kuantung, Kuangsi and Furhien provinces, prior to establishing recruiting contacts in the north. However, the failure of the southern recruiting drive as a result of the opposition of local vested interests working for the Malaysian emigrant trade and the poor quality of recruits secured from the south, meant that after November 1904 all of the industry’s attention was focused on Shantung and Chihli.[55] The wisdom and efficacy of exploiting the maximum area of the labour market was thereby strongly emphasised, for despite initial difficulties, recruits were drawn in from a wide area of the interior of these two most populous provinces of the north China plain. The essential reason for the success of the CMLIA in the north must be sought in its appreciation of the market opportunities provided by the closure of Manchurian emigration as a result of the Russo-Japanese War of 1904-5. This removed the largest source of competitive demand for labour and enabled the CMLIA to tap an already established migratory movement.[56]
 Large scale recruiting in the northern provinces initiated by the CMLIA was matched by large scale shipment and embarkation. From the outset, in June 1904, embarkation from the north was concentrated on the port of Taku [Tientsin] but was transferred north to Chinwangtao in August 1904. This was a much safer anchorage, facilitated the free use of large steamers, provided better and cheaper accommodation facilities for the intending recruits, and was further away from the depredations of rival recruiters. Embarkation from Shantung did not begin until November 1904, when Chefoo became a regular port of call for South Africa bound steamers.[57]
 In the provision of transport, the CMLIA was materially aided by the impact of steam technology upon the passenger trade of the Far East. Its application to South African importation ensured that a large scale cost effective arrangement could be extended from the interior through to the


-----

##### landing of recruits in Durban. Steamers materially enlarged the range of passenger voyages, whilst simultaneously increasing the speed at which these be undertaken. This was a major consideration, particularly when recruiting centred upon the northern provinces, as this added an additional five days to the thirty day trip from Hong Kong to Durban. Further, the considerably increased capacity which steam shipping permitted, reduced unit costs without increasing risk or loss through wastage. The thirty-two voyages from north China ports undertaken between June 1904 and November 1906 had an average passenger complement of 1, 945, and an average death rate of 0.004 per 1, 000 embarked. The introduction of steam shipping also greatly increased the rate of turnover of shipping on the voyage. This kept the need for a large number of ships on charter to a minimum. This can be seen most clearly in the shipping arrangements for 1906 when the last two voyages to South Africa from China were made by the same ship - the S.S. Cranley. Indeed, this vessel succeeded in making no less than four round trips from China to South Africa in 1906, a feat which would have been impossible in the days of sail. The CMLIA was also aided in the conduct of this extensive operation by developments in telegraphic communication. Rapid control over the finer details of the shipping and recruiting of labourers was made possible by telegraphic communication within the CMLIA and between the FLD in Johannesburg and its Transvaal Emigration Agents.[58]
 The CMLIA adopted policies at the embarkation ports, on board ship, and at the port of arrival in Natal designed to capitalise upon this situation. These policies essentially involved the employment of strict medical criteria to weed out recruits considered unsuitable for mining work by doctors employed by the CMLIA and the subsequent imposition of a strict code of discipline to maintain an acceptable level of public health on board ship and in the depots. This policy was backed up by the introduction of internal policing methods imposed by the companies not only to reinforce the medical regime but also to accustom the workforce to the social control mechanisms operative on the mines. This force of ‘Chinese Police’ was chosen from amongst the recruits at the port of embarkation and placed under the control of a European overseer on board ship. Subject to satisfactory conduct on the voyage, these police would be confirmed in their position on the mines, whilst in many cases the overseers would take up the position of Compound Manager at the mine.[59]


-----

##### Up to the point of transfer of the imported labourers from the CMLIA to the individual mining companies the industry had been involved in substantial outlays of money to secure a labour force of sufficient size and quality at the right price. Thus, whilst an average of £17/5/2 per head was expanded as non-recoverable costs of importation, these were in addition to the cost of share allocations in the Agency and the capital costs involved in the alteration of compound buildings to meet the standards laid down by the government.[60] To deal with this situation, certain very clear patterns of employment were devised by the mining industry, with the result that Chinese labour differed in some important respects from other forms of indentured labour employed in the mines at this period.
 The most notable difference between Chinese and African unskilled labour was the greatly increased period of service. A minimum time of three years was laid down in the Chinese indenture, with an option to renew for further periods not to exceed five years in total. This initial period of service was considered the minimum period within which the mining companies could recover their outlay on importation costs, taking into account the ‘wastage’ of labourers resulting from deaths, injuries, desertion or repatriations.[61] Two additional advantages were also specified in the Chinese contract of service which gave the mines the advantages they sought to reverse the crisis in accumulation. First, the contract of service stipulated that the Chinese could only be employed in metalliferous mining. Second, that such employment had to be within the Witwatersrand district.[62] This meant in effect that the labour of the Chinese work force was exclusively at the disposal of the gold mining industry for the full period of its employment. Competition for Chinese labour within the Transvaal, either from any other type of mining outside the Witwatersrand, or from any other type of business anywhere in the Transvaal, was illegal. In the absence of competition amongst this restricted group of employers, an unlikely threat in view of the arrangements entered into, the gold mining industry was able to determine the allocation of labour in a manner best suited to its requirements.
 The industry sought to exploit the advantages that it derived from this captive labour force also in the arrangements that it made for the distribution of importees as they arrived from China. To meet the demand, the CMLIA allocated the complements of African and Chinese labour separately, on a pro rata basis to match the complements of the individual


-----

##### mines controlled by particular Groups. Initially, it distributed approximately 2, 200 Chinese labourers to each Group and then, after some readjustment, distributed the Chinese to Groups having the least percentage of their shareholding in the CMLIA represented by Chinese labourers. On this basis, two batches of 500 labourers from the same ship were allocated to a Group should its percentage, after taking into account its first 500, be lower than another Group which was an applicant for Chinese at the same time.[63]
 There were two important consequences of these arrangements which tended to increase the division amongst the mining houses into richer and poorer Groups. First, it tended to limit the extent to which Groups not able to afford Chinese labour could expect to benefit directly from the aggregate increase in the labour supply which resulted from importation, as distribution of African labour was not based on the number of Chinese held by a particular group. Second, this distribution deliberately favoured the richer and more powerful Groups by allocating Chinese labour on the basis of their differential subscription of capital to the CMLIA.
 Within this differential commitment to Chinese labour, the clear demarcation between it and other groups of unskilled labour was extended by a greater than average distribution of Chinese to the producing mines of all Groups. This was a deliberate policy, for unlike the rules governing the distribution of labour in WNLA, there was no obligation to give stamping mines priority in the allocation of labour under the CMLIA’s rules on distribution.[64] This policy was extended by a greater than average distribution of Chinese miners on underground work in the producing mines in which they were engaged.[65] One reason for this was that despite an original intention to the contrary, there was no policy to differentiate between so-called Chinese and African mines.[66] What this meant in practice was that batches of Chinese imported by a particular company were supplemented by groups of African largely for surface work. The varying numbers depended on the mine complement and the availability of African and Chinese labour at the time. The idea of segregation was abandoned because the large number of Chinese introduced made it impossible to keep labourers apart indefinitely. Furthermore, there were strong financial grounds for integration. Apart from the obvious disadvantages of reorganisation resulting from a mine being stripped of its work force to make way for another, many mines were not, especially at first, in a position ‘to spend enormous sums of money obtaining full complements of


-----

##### coolies’.[67] As a result, only three out of a total of fifty-four companies employed Chinese labour exclusively in all six years of the use of this type of labour.[68]
 To reinforce this pattern of labour distribution, the Groups tended to encourage a distinct move towards concentration in occupational areas in particular types of mines where recuperation of importation costs was likely to be most rapid and where Chinese labour was most useful in reducing working costs and raising output through increased efficiency. A logical extension of this policy was to employ a very high proportion of Chinese miners in the strategically most productive jobs. Of these undoubtedly the most important was that of hand drilling in the stopes. No less than 65-75 per cent of all Chinese employed on the Rand were used in this way.[69] To effect this, widespread use of piece work payment was introduced. Productivity related payments were undoubtedly much more widespread for Chinese than African labour at the time, and were introduced not only because of the necessity of keeping costs to a minimum and recuperating high importation charges, but also by the provisions of Clause 6 of the contract which stipulated a minimum rate for day’s pay after a six month period. As these conditions did not apply to piece work, the industry had a double incentive to introduce the practice of labour.[70]
 The timing of the introduction of piece-work contracts was determined by the realisation that Clause 6 had put strong bargaining power into the hands of the Chinese work force. Following a major confrontation between the mine management and the CMLIA at the North Randfontein gold mine in April 1905, it became imperative for the management to introduce a system of payment which removed the necessity of extracting surplus through a hierarchy of unreliable intermediaries, and simultaneously ensured a retention of management control over productivity and wage rates.[71] As early as January 1905 the Committee of Consulting Engineers had drawn up a list of piece work rates applicable to nearly 80 per cent of Chinese occupations. As the Chinese moved out of their initial periods of employment, more and more of these types of agreements were engaged on the Rand after April 1905.[72]
 These measures had a two-fold effect. First, they were largely successful in containing the wage costs of Chinese labour whilst rapidly increasing output. Between 1904 and 1910, mining expenses increased by 99.7 per


-----

##### cent, and revenue by 99.03 per cent. As a proportion of these gross expenses, aggregate Chinese wages represented an average of 4.27 per cent of the total every year between 1904 and 1909.[73] At the same time, the effect of these productivity-related payments on industrial relations was particularly bad. Piece work became a major source of grievance for the Chinese work force, as the mine managements made persistent attempts to extract greater and greater benefits from the terms of the contracts. In one notorious case, at the Cason gold mine on the East Rand in 1907, a dispute over the interpretation of a piece work contract ended in loss of life and serious damage to property.[74] Although the mining industry was able to overcome the disruptive effects of these disputes by increased collaboration with the state, the political impact of worsening relations between the Chinese and their employers was detrimental to the long-term future of importation.[75]
 Despite this temporary worsening of relations between Chinese labour and the mine managements, importation soon had a material impact on the crisis in the mining industry. Thus, Chinese labour made a major contribution to the absolute growth of the labour force. This was particularly rapid in the period of bulk importation between June 1904 and February 1905, but continued throughout the period of importation down to January 1907. During the whole period of importation, the unskilled labour force as a whole increased from 69, 336 to 135, 059.[76] Much of this change was directly attributable to the Chinese as can be seen by the fact that at one point, in July 1906, Chinese labour represented 40.22 per cent of labour at work, the largest single group of workers in the mines. Concurrently the gathering recession in the Transvaal initiated a parallel process in the numbers of African miners at work.[77]
 At the same time Chinese importation tended to reduce substantially the impact of fluctuations in the supply of African labour, and had a marked effect upon the rate of labour turnover.[78] Thus, in the two years 1904-6, the turnover rate of the whole unskilled labour force fell by 61.8 per cent, of which only 5 per cent was attributable to increases in the length of service of African miners.[79] Even more significant, this increasingly favourable position was achieved at a time of stabilising wage costs for African labour. Although Chinese importation did not succeed in reversing the upward trend in African wage rates until 1906-7, it certainly slowed the rate of


-----

##### increase dramatically after the very steep rises of 1901-4.[80] At the same time, Chinese labour also obviated the necessity of finding any alternative source of unskilled labour, particularly more expensive white unskilled labour, and moved the all-important ratio of white to coloured labour sharply in favour of cheaper coloured labour.[81] Chinese importation also effectively eliminated the more expensive and marginal sources of African labour, such as Central Africa and German South West Africa.[82]
 Chinese labour also made a major contribution to the long-term structural transformation of the gold mining industry. This involved an increased rate of production and the maintenance of a differential capital composition in the production process at a time of increased scale of operations. The ability of the mining industry to employ large numbers of Chinese miners on the productively strategic tasks of hand rock drilling extended the stoping area available to individual mines, whilst narrowing the size of individual stopes worked. By increasing the output of cleaner ore, this process controlled the lowering of the grade of ore mined. At the same time, by allowing for more hand drilling and sorting, the reduced volume of waste in the ore lowered working costs to compensate for the lower grade rock. Costs were also lowered by more selective use of rock drills, facilitating the replacement of expensive white machine-drill operators from areas where hand drilling was more practical and economic, and by increasing capital investment in surface operations.[83]
 Yet it would be wrong to stress the role of the Chinese in reversing the post-war crisis in the mining industry to the exclusion of other factors for the simple reason that the full potential of this labour was never allowed to develop. From mid-1907 the wholesale repatriation of the Chinese labour force began under the terms of the Indentured Labour Laws (Temporary Continuance) Act of the new Transvaal Parliament. This law, which effectively terminated the importation of Chinese labour and ensured its eventual demise as a part of the unskilled labour complement of the mining industry was the culmination of a crisis which began in December 1905. In that month, the fall of Balfour’s Conservative and Unionist Government resulted in the advent of a Liberal administration under Campbell- Bannerman committed to immediate cessation of importation and wholesale repatriation of the Chinese work force. Despite promises made during their victorious electoral campaign in January 1906, the Liberal government was not able to force the issue on either repatriation or importation. The


-----

##### potential cost of annulling the validity of outstanding importation licenses to which Transvaal government approval had been given, forced the administration to permit the continuation of importation and recruitment until 30 November 1906. As a result, a large proportion of the importation licenses issued were taken up by the mining industry. At the same time, the disruptive effects of immediate repatriation also persuaded the Liberal government to draw back from this proposal. Instead, it permitted the honouring of existing arrangements pending the grant of Responsible Government under terms of new Letters Patent which effectively provided for the termination of the system of Chinese indentured labour.[84]
 Under terms of Article L(I) of the new Transvaal Constitution, the right of renewal of the contract of service was withdrawn. Under Article L(2) it was provided that within one year of the meeting of the Transvaal Legislature that the Importation Ordinance and all amending Ordinances Rules and Regulations would cease to have legality within the Colony. This proposal, by threatening to turn the Chinese into free labourers, ensured that Chinese labour would be the main issue of the forthcoming election. The victory of Botha’s Het Volk party committed to Chinese repatriation did not result, however, in a continuation of the Liberal government’s entire programme. The new Transvaal government responded to representations from the mining industry that to repatriate the Chinese without time being given to find adequate substitutes would be ‘a recipe for chaos’.[85]
 Consequently, the Het Volk government passed the Indentured Labour Laws (Temporary Continuance) Act to suspend the operation of Article L(2) of the Letters Patent, whilst confirming Article L(1). As a result, Chinese repatriation began as contracts expired in the second half of 1907 and continued until February 1910, when the last shipment of time-expired labourers left Durban for China.[86]
 Between 1907 and 1910, the mining industry was able to make an effective and long lasting return to reliance on African unskilled labour. Nevertheless the pattern of utilisation was different. Prior to the introduction of the Chinese there had been marked dependence upon indentured labour from Portuguese East Africa. After 1907, the proportion of African labour on shorter contracts drawn from within the British South African High Commission territories showed a substantial and continuing rise. This change was made in the context of a significantly enlarged work force. Central to an understanding of this process is an appreciation of the


-----

##### importance of the Transvaal government’s establishment of the Native Labour Bureau in 1907. This institution was set up to aid the mining industry’s transfer back to African labour by facilitating the flow of labour from the Cape Colony, Natal and Zululand. As a result of the Bureau’s activities, there was an 87.2 per cent increase in African labour at work in the mines from the Cape and a 54.5 per cent increase from Natal and Zululand in the period December 1906-1908.[87] The explanation for this development must be sought in the underlying economic situation. By 1907, the country had been in the grip of an economic recession for close on four years. The severity of the downturn was such that as early as 1905 it had made a noticeable impact on the industry’s available African labour supply. At that time the continuation of the Chinese supply had led to the rejection of surplus African labour. The continuation of this situation, poor harvests, and related structural changes in many African communities had turned the situation decisively in the industry’s favour by the time that repatriation became a reality. Thus, at the height of the Chinese exodus in March 1908, the Chamber of Mines was able to report that the unskilled labour position ‘is on a very satisfactory footing at the present.’[88]
 Even as this shift in the pattern of African labour was establishing itself, the traditional predominance of the southern districts of Portuguese East Africa was itself under challenge as the industry began large scale recruiting in the tropical areas of the territory, and in the neighbouring British Central African Protectorate.[89] This drive into the tropical territories, with a consequent rapid rise in the death rate of African miners employed, was to be one of the bitterest legacies of this controversial period in the history of the Transvaal gold mining industry.[90]

Ordinances 27 of 1905, see Transvaal, A Handbook of Ordinances, pp.23 et seq.; some of the powers conferred on inspectors by this Ordinance were curtailed by an additional Importation Amendment Act of 1906. This Ordinance, No.12 of 1906, was passed on instruction of the new Liberal government in London. Despite its scaling down of inspectors’ powers, the principle of allowing the Executive’s officers the right to exercise judicial powers was retained.
